                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2014 32:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-09-14"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sequencing and de novo assembly of seven wild relatives of soybean yields insights relevant to crop domestication and improvement. Wild relatives of crops are an important source of genetic diversity for agriculture, but their gene repertoire remains largely unexplored. We report the establishment and analysis of a pan-genome of Glycine soja, the wild relative of cultivated soybean Glycine max, by sequencing and de novo assembly of seven phylogenetically and geographically representative accessions. Intergenomic comparisons identified lineage-specific genes and genes with copy number variation or large-effect mutations, some of which show evidence of positive selection and may contribute to variation of agronomic traits such as biotic resistance, seed composition, flowering and maturity time, organ size and final biomass. Approximately 80% of the pan-genome was present in all seven accessions (core), whereas the rest was dispensable and exhibited greater variation than the core genome, perhaps reflecting a role in adaptation to diverse environments. This work will facilitate the harnessing of untapped genetic diversity from wild soybean for enhancement of elite cultivars."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Wild relatives of crops are an important source of genetic diversity for agriculture, but their gene repertoire remains largely unexplored. We report the establishment and analysis of a pan-genome of <i>Glycine soja</i>, the wild relative of cultivated soybean <i>Glycine max</i>, by sequencing and <i>de novo</i> assembly of seven phylogenetically and geographically representative accessions. Intergenomic comparisons identified lineage-specific genes and genes with copy number variation or large-effect mutations, some of which show evidence of positive selection and may contribute to variation of agronomic traits such as biotic resistance, seed composition, flowering and maturity time, organ size and final biomass. Approximately 80% of the pan-genome was present in all seven accessions (core), whereas the rest was dispensable and exhibited greater variation than the core genome, perhaps reflecting a role in adaptation to diverse environments. This work will facilitate the harnessing of untapped genetic diversity from wild soybean for enhancement of elite cultivars.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Annual wild soybean (<i>Glycine soja</i> Sieb. &amp; Zucc.) is the closest relative and antecedent of cultivated soybean (<i>Glycine max</i> (L.) Merr.), one of the world's primary sources of plant protein and vegetable oil. Whereas <i>G. max</i> has lost substantial genetic diversity through successive bottlenecks owing to domestication and selection for traits to increase yield under intensive human cultivation, <i>G. soja</i> is distributed across a broad geographical range (24â53Â° N, 97â143Â° E) and has adapted to a variety of ecological conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Qiu, L.J. et al. A platform for soybean molecular breeding: the utilization of core collections for food security. Plant Mol. Biol. 83, 41â50 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47811e883">1</a></sup>, providing a promising source of novel genes for soybean improvement needed to respond to rapid population growth and environmental changes.</p><p>Since the publication of the soybean genome (var. Williams 82 (GmaxW82))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d47811e890">2</a></sup>, attempts have been made to characterize <i>G. soja</i> through genome resequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053â1059 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47811e897">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e900">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Molecular footprints of domestication and improvement in soybean revealed by whole genome re-sequencing. BMC Genomics 14, 579 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d47811e903">5</a></sup>. However, resequencing is limited in terms of capturing many types of structural variation, especially for crop wild relatives, which are more genetically diverse than their domesticated counterparts. High levels of sequence similarity are required to map resequencing short reads to a reference genome at the expense of losing information from more diverged genomic regions. Presence-absence variation (PAV) and copy number variation (CNV), which are often associated with agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hufford, M.B. et al. Comparative population genomics of maize domestication and improvement. Nat. Genet. 44, 808â811 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d47811e907">6</a></sup>, may also be missed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, R. et al. Building the sequence map of the human pan-genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 57â63 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d47811e911">7</a></sup>. Moreover, a single genome is insufficient to represent the genomic content of a predominantly selfing (autogamous) species, such as <i>G. soja</i>, in which individuals are distinct from one another due to low levels of genetic exchange and recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Genetic diversity in domesticated soybean (Glycine max) and its wild progenitor (Glycine soja) for simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphism loci. New Phytol. 188, 242â253 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47811e919">8</a></sup>. Thus, the <i>de novo</i> construction of a pan-genome for a species, consisting of a core genome shared among individuals and individual-specific or partially shared dispensable genome, is necessary to capture the majority of genetic diversity within a species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Tettelin, H. et al. Genome analysis of multiple pathogenic isolates of Streptococcus agalactiae: implications for the microbial 'pan-genome'. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13950â13955 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d47811e926">9</a></sup>.</p><p>To fully characterize the genomic content and molecular evolutionary history of <i>G. soja</i>, the undomesticated gene pool of soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Genetic diversity in domesticated soybean (Glycine max) and its wild progenitor (Glycine soja) for simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphism loci. New Phytol. 188, 242â253 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47811e936">8</a></sup>, we constructed a pan-genome by sequencing and assembling seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions <i>de novo</i>. Within this pan-genome, the dispensable gene set was found to have evolved more rapidly and be more variable than the core gene set, and genes under selection exhibited relatively little overlap between the seven <i>G. soja</i> lineages, indicating that local adaptation may have affected nonoverlapping sets of genes. Some of the genes with structural variation between <i>G. soja</i> and GmaxW82, based on homology and co-localization with mapped quantitative trait loci (QTLs), may be associated with adaptation to various abiotic and biotic stresses. Our data also push the divergence between <i>G. max</i> and <i>G. soja</i> back to <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.8 million years ago (mya), from a recent estimate of <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.27 mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e959">4</a></sup>. This work illustrates the value of <i>de novo</i> assemblies in building and characterizing large eukaryotic pan-genomes, and provides candidate genes for introgression into cultivated soybean for crop improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>De novo</i> genome assemblies of seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions</h3><p>Seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions, representing the geographical adaptation within the species, were selected and named GsojaA to GsojaG (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Six accessions represent major genetically distinct clusters (North, Huanghuai and South regions of China, and Japan, Korea and Russia) of <i>G. soja</i> and 87.1% of the genetic diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Genetic diversity in domesticated soybean (Glycine max) and its wild progenitor (Glycine soja) for simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphism loci. New Phytol. 188, 242â253 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1000">8</a></sup>. Another was selected from the Northeast region (NER) of China, a predicted soybean domestication center<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fukuda, Y. Cytological studies on the wild and cultivated Manchurian soybeans. Jap. J. Bot. 6, 489â506 (1933)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1004">10</a></sup>, to better represent the geographic distribution of <i>G. soja</i>. Each genome was sequenced with Illumina HiSeq2000 using a combination of libraries with insert sizes of 180 bp, 500 bp and 2 Kbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 2</a>), for an average of 111.9 fold coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The estimated genome sizes ranged from 889.33 Mbp for GsojaG (93.6% of the GmaxW82) to 1,118.34 Mbp for GsojaD (117.7% of GmaxW82). Variation in genome size could be partially explained by differences in abundance of repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Sample information, assembly and annotation for seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2979/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Each genome was assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1551">11</a></sup>, a <i>de novo</i> genome assembler based on a de Bruijn graph algorithm, and resulted in contig N50 sizes ranging from 8 to 27 Kbp and scaffold N50 sizes ranging from 17 to 65.1 Kbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The assemblies were validated by alignment to GmaxW82, covering more than 94% of the 54,175 genes (Glyma1.1 annotation, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net">http://www.phytozome.net</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The coverage of GmaxW82 genes by assembled genomes was either higher than (5 <i>G. soja</i> genomes) or comparable to (2 <i>G. soja</i> genomes) the coverage by aligning resequencing reads. As such, the current assemblies enabled accurate detection of variation and comparative analyses within genic regions. Additional mate-pair libraries with large inserts would further improve assembly size, thereby resolving variation in nongenic regions.</p><p>Protein-coding genes were annotated for each genome by integrating homology searches, mRNA expression evidence, and <i>ab initio</i> prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 3</a>). After correction for gene fragments due to incomplete assembly, we estimated an average of 55,570 genes per genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Tab1">Table 1</a>), slightly more than the 54,175 genes in GmaxW82. The quality of the annotation was supported by the observation that 89.24â91.93% of genes had at least one ortholog in the GmaxW82 genome and 63.94â71.19% of the predicted genes were expressed, as determined by RNA-seq of mixed tissues of corresponding accessions. In addition, a majority (96.15â97.16%) had protein homologs in other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Underestimation of gene number may result from lack of evidence to support prediction. More likely, however, the gene number may be inflated as it includes genes split across contigs and genes on separately assembled haplotypes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Variation between <i>G. soja</i> and GmaxW82</h3><p>To identify variation between <i>G. soja</i> genomes and GmaxW82, we designed a computational pipeline that takes advantage of the sequencing reads as well as assembled genomes to catalog variation including single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions or deletions (indels), CNV and PAV (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). There were 3.63â4.72 million SNPs in individual samples, with 0.12â0.15 million in coding sequence regions (CDS). This included 1,764 loci where <i>G. soja</i> had a stop codon and GmaxW82 did not, and 2,286 loci where GmaxW82 had a stop codon and <i>G. soja</i> did not (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 5</a>). The assembly-based method enabled us to find more SNPs than by resequencing alone, especially in divergent regions where unassembled short sequencing reads are difficult to be mapped (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Figs. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">6</a>). Even though <i>G. soja</i> is a predominantly selfing species, three of the seven (GsojaA, GsojaC and GsojaD) had relatively high heterozygous SNP rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 5</a>). The high rate of heterozygosity may have complicated <i>de novo</i> assembly and resulted in inflated gene numbers due to divergent haplotypes being represented as separate scaffolds in the assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Examples of variation between G. soja and G. max."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Examples of variation between <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="609"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) A diverged region on chromosome 15. Top panel, coverage of GmaxW82 (blue) and GsojaA genome (red) by GsojaA reads. Comparison between GmaxW82 and <i>G. soja</i> sequences show nine SNPs in a 62-bp fragment at bottom. This highly diverged region could not be aligned by mapping GsojaA reads to GmaxW82. (<b>b</b>) Indels in <i>Glyma02g25230.</i> GmaxW82 gene structure is shown at top (exons in blue) and five amino acid changes caused by three indels in <i>G. soja</i> are shown in red at bottom. (<b>c</b>) <i>G. sojaâ</i>specific PAV. Syntenic genomic region on chromosome 14 (Block A) compared to its homolog on chromosome 02 (Block B). Genes in each block are numbered A1 to A7 and B1 to B8, respectively. An 8-Kbp <i>G. sojaâ</i>specific region is outlined in red, in which the three genes absent in the <i>G. max</i> region but with homologs on block A are in yellow, and the two genes with no homologs either on block A or elsewhere in <i>G. max</i> are in purple. Genes exhibiting relatively conserved synteny between species are marked in blue. The anchor point of each block is shown in black. Homologous genes are connected by magenta shading and the anchor points of each block are connected by gray shading. A phylogenetic tree was built using genes in red (<i>Glyma02g38310</i>). (<b>d</b>) <i>G. maxâ</i>specific PAV. Depth distribution of <i>G. soja</i> genomes on GmaxW82 chromosome 15 (46â46.5 Mbp) is shown with <i>y</i> axis representing the depth ratio on top and GC content below. The sequence depth ratio was calculated using the average depth in 10-Kbp windows for each <i>G. soja</i> accession over the genome-wide average. Seven of the 19 genes with low read depth in <i>G. soja</i> accessions were identified as <i>G. maxâ</i>specific genes, for example, <i>Glyma15g39573</i>. (<b>e</b>) Distribution of genetic variation across the soybean genome. A, chromosome ideograms for <i>G. max</i>, pericentromeric regions represented in gray; B, gene density; C, distribution of genes containing large-effect mutations; D, CNV (red for gain, green for loss); and E, <i>G. maxâ</i>specific PAV. For circle C, D and E, numbers represent percentages of genes with corresponding mutations over total genes in 1-Mbp window.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We detected 0.50â0.77 million indels in <i>G. soja</i> as compared to GmaxW82, of which 93.3% (70/75) of randomly selected indel alleles were validated by Sanger sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Figs. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">8</a>). Although many indels were trinucleotides, 2,989â4,181 resulted in frameshifts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Tables 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">8</a>). For example, three indels were identified in one of the two homologs of <i>Spiral2</i> (<i>spr2</i>) (<i>AT4G27060</i>), a key microtubule gene for directional cell elongation that is associated with the right-handed helical growth in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shoji, T. et al. Plant-specific microtubule-associated protein SPIRAL2 is required for anisotropic growth in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 136, 3933â3944 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1772">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). In <i>Glyma02g25230</i>, the <i>Spiral2</i>-homolog, three guanines were deleted at positions 695, 707 and 714 in all seven <i>G. soja</i> genomes, resulting in five amino acid changes in one of the HEAT-repeat motifs. Thus, <i>Glyma02g25230</i> is a candidate gene that may be responsible for the change from the twining growth habit found in <i>G. soja</i> to erect growth found in <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>).</p><p>We identified CNV in genic regions and found hundreds of genes that had either gained or lost copies in individual <i>G. soja</i> accessions compared to GmaxW82. Of 1,978 genes affected in the <i>G. soja</i> accessions, 1,179 had CNV loss, 726 had CNV gain and 73 had both CNV loss and gain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Tables 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">10</a>). Gene Ontology (GO) analysis indicated that genes related to abiotic and biotic stress tolerance, such as resistance (R)-genes with nucleotide-binding site (NBS) or NBS-leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domains and transcription factors, were significantly (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, chi-squared test) enriched in genes affected by CNV. This indicates that these genes are evolutionarily labile and may be involved in adaptation to local environments and/or interaction with pathogens.</p><p>In total, 2.3â3.9 Mbp of <i>G. soja</i>âspecific PAV (defined as &gt;100 bp and &lt;95% identity) was present in the <i>G. soja</i> genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 11</a>), less than the 8.3 Mbp reported for a single previously resequenced <i>G. soja</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1834">4</a></sup>. Our lower PAV discovery rate may be due to the use of more stringent filtering criteria, including the removal of unassembled sequences in <i>G. max</i> and putative microbial sequences. There were 338 genes found to have at least 50% of their coding sequences composed of <i>G. soja</i>âspecific sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Of these genes or genomic loci affected by putative PAV, 37 were selected for PCR-based genotyping across GmaxW82 and the seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions (using 43 primer pairs). All 37 loci were concordant with the predicted PAV that was present in <i>G. soja</i> and absent in <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Tables 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Of the <i>G. soja</i>âspecific PAV genes, 34 were inferred to be involved in defense response, 33 in cell growth and 15 in photosynthesis, by GO category enrichment analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p><p>As an example of genic PAV, an 8-Kbp <i>G. soja</i>âspecific region was identified in all seven accessions (Block A) on chromosome 14 and validated by PCR genotyping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). A paralogous region (Block B) on chromosome 02 in <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max</i> contained three genes missing in Block A that were related to biotic and abiotic stress tolerance or plant development (<i>Glyma02g38290</i>, homolog of <i>ZRZ/ZF14</i> or <i>BCD1</i>; <i>Glyma02g38300</i>, homolog of <i>SUT4</i> and <i>Glyma02g38310</i>, homolog of <i>GLY1</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chanda, B. et al. Glycerol-3-phosphate is a critical mobile inducer of systemic immunity in plants. Nat. Genet. 43, 421â427 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1915">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Weise, A. et al. A new subfamily of sucrose transporters, SUT4, with low affinity/high capacity localized in enucleate sieve elements of plants. Plant Cell 12, 1345â1355 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1918">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Of the 56 resequenced <i>Glycine</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053â1059 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1928">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1931">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Molecular footprints of domestication and improvement in soybean revealed by whole genome re-sequencing. BMC Genomics 14, 579 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1934">5</a></sup>, 17 of 27 <i>G. soja</i> accessions and three of 29 <i>G. max</i> accessions contained this putative <i>G. sojaâ</i>specific sequence, indicating that this PAV is segregating in both <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max</i> but the frequency has been reduced in <i>G. max</i>, perhaps due to the domestication bottleneck.</p><p>The total length of <i>G. maxâ</i>specific PAV (1.86 Mbp) was less than that of <i>G. sojaâ</i>specific PAV. The GC content for these regions (32.9%) was not significantly different from the whole genome average (34.1%), rejecting the possibility of sequencing bias. A total of 16 genes were identified to contain <i>G. maxâ</i>specific sequences (&gt;50% of the length of the CDS affected), including four genes that were entirely <i>G. maxâ</i>specific (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). This number was less than the 712 found in an earlier comparison of a single <i>G. soja</i> accession and GmaxW82 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e1978">4</a>), which may be due to increased sampling of <i>G. soja</i> diversity and more stringent criteria. Based on KEGG analysis of the four <i>G. maxâ</i>specific genes, <i>Glyma01g37051</i> is involved in energy metabolism (ko00190 and ko00194), <i>Glyma13g36351</i> and <i>Glyma15g39541</i> in lipid metabolism (ko00590, ko00591 and ko00592), and <i>Glyma15g39596</i> in cell growth and death (ko04210). <i>Glyma01g37051</i> is a homolog of <i>AtLFNR1</i> (ferredoxin-NADP<sup>+</sup>-oxidoreductase, <i>AT5G66190</i>), which in <i>Arabidopsis</i> is involved in photosystem Iâdependent cyclic electron now and affects rosette size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lintala, M. et al. Structural and functional characterization of ferredoxin-ADP+-xidoreductase using knock-out mutants of Arabidopsis. Plant J. 49, 1041â1052 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2016">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Variation was distributed unevenly throughout the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>). Genes in recombination-suppressed pericentromeric regions were more significantly (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, chi-squared test) affected than those in chromosome arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 16</a>), as was also observed in <i>Zea mays</i> (maize)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Swanson-Wagner, R.A. et al. Pervasive gene content variation and copy number variation in maize and its undomesticated progenitor. Genome Res. 20, 1689â1699 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2035">16</a></sup>. The majority of genes affected by large-effect variations (including SNPs or indels causing stop codon gain or loss and frameshift), CNV or genome-specific sequences were rare events usually found in only one of the seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions. These rare variants may be useful in improving soybean cultivars through breeding, as has been indicated by studies in maize and wheat, in which rare alleles were found to be associated with QTLs for agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Genome-wide genetic changes during modern breeding of maize. Nat. Genet. 44, 812â815 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2043">17</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of the <i>G. max</i> and <i>G. soja</i> species complex</h3><p>We next constructed a phylogenetic tree using 670 conserved, single-copy, gene orthologs from <i>G. soja</i>, <i>G. max</i> and three other sequenced legume species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Using the divergence time between <i>Medicago</i> and <i>Glycine</i> as a calibration point<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Arakaki, M. et al. Contemporaneous and recent radiations of the world's major succulent plant lineages. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 8379â8384 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2078">18</a></sup>, we estimated that pigeon pea and soybean diverged <span class="stix">â¼</span>23 mya, and common bean and soybean <span class="stix">â¼</span>29.5 mya, similar to previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Arakaki, M. et al. Contemporaneous and recent radiations of the world's major succulent plant lineages. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 8379â8384 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2082">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2085">19</a></sup>. We also estimated the sequenced <i>G. soja</i> accessions and <i>G. max</i> diverged <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.8 mya, earlier than previously estimated (0.27 mya) based on a single <i>G. soja</i> accession<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2098">4</a></sup>. This difference is likely due to the inclusion of more diverged <i>G. soja</i> accessions in the current study.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Divergence among G. max, G. soja and selected legumes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Divergence among <i>G. max</i>, <i>G. soja</i> and selected legumes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="345"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Left, Relationship and divergence times among sequenced legume species, including <i>Cajanus cajan</i>, <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i>. Right, relationship of sequenced <i>G. soja</i> accessions and <i>G. max</i> (GmaxW82).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The divergence time between <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max</i> predates the estimated domestication time of soybean of <span class="stix">â¼</span>5,500 ya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lee, G.A., Crawford, G.W., Liu, L., Sasaki, Y. &amp; Chen, X. Archaeological soybean (Glycine max) in East Asia: does size matter? PLoS ONE 6, e26720 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2157">20</a></sup>, therefore divergent selection may have contributed to the differentiation of the two subspecies before domestication of <i>G. max</i>. Using the branch-site likelihood ratio test, we identified 682 genes that showed evidence of positive selection in the GmaxW82 genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 17</a>). These genes were enriched in diverse biological functional categories related to abiotic stress regulation, including stomatal complex development, voltage-gated potassium channel activity, proline metabolism and nitrate transport (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 18</a>). In the <i>G. soja</i> accessions, 175â408 such genes were identified in individual wild soybean accessions and none was shared among all accessions. Only ten positive-selection genes were shared by at least three <i>G. soja</i> accessions and 132 such genes were shared by two accessions. Genes that underwent positive selection may have contributed to the adaptation of <i>G. soja</i> accessions to different environments, as evidenced by the species' wide range of geographical distribution and lack of shared positive-selection genes.</p><p>Changes within the <i>G. soja</i> pan-genome (total gene set) and core genome (genes shared among all seven genomes) were analyzed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). The number of total genes increased as additional genomes were added and, in contrast, the number of shared genes decreased with additional <i>G. soja</i> genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). The average pan-genome size of any two accessions accounted for 78.2% of that found using all seven accessions, confirming that a single genome does not adequately represent the diversity contained within <i>G. soja</i>. The pan-genome size was asymptotic with size of the seven genomes and an initial <i>G. soja</i> pan-genome with 59,080 gene families and an overall size 986.3 Mbp was constructed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Nearly half (48.6%) of the gene families and 80.1% of sequences conserved across all seven <i>G. soja</i> genomes were core genomic units (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Figs. 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">15</a>). Approximately half of the gene families (51.4% or 30,364) were present in more than one, but not all seven <i>G. soja</i> genomes, and represent the dispensable genome. As might be expected, unique gene families, those found only in one sample, were the least frequent and, among the seven accessions, GsojaD had the greatest amount of accession-specific gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="G.soja pan-genome components and evolution of core and dispensable genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: <i>G.soja</i> pan-genome components and evolution of core and dispensable genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="341"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Increase and decrease in gene families in pan-genome and core genome, respectively, with every additional soja genome. (<b>b</b>) Frequency of indels and SNPs (left panel) and <i>dn</i>, <i>ds</i> and <i>dn/ds</i> (right) in core and dispensable genomes. **<i>P</i> = 0.001, <i>t</i>-test. Error bars, mean Â± s.d.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Owing to recent acquisition and deletion events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Donati, C. et al. Structure and dynamics of the pan-genome of Streptococcus pneumoniae and closely related species. Genome Biol. 11, R107 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2272">21</a></sup>, the dispensable gene set was more variable than the core gene set. The frequency of SNPs in the core gene set was estimated to be 2.67 sites per Kbp, significantly less than 4.12/Kbp in the dispensable gene set (<i>t</i>-test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01). The same bias was observed in the frequency of indels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Moreover, the dispensable genes had significantly higher <i>dn</i>, <i>ds</i> and <i>dn</i>/<i>ds</i> ratio values than did core genes (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, <i>t</i>-test; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), indicating that the dispensable genes have undergone weaker purifying selection and/or greater positive selection than core genes. Core genes were enriched in biological processes including growth, immune system processes, reproduction, reproductive processes, cellular processes, and cellular component organization or biogenesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 21</a>). Three categories were enriched in dispensable genes: receptor activity, structural molecule activity and antioxidant activity in molecular function. The core genes were more functionally conserved than the dispensable genes: 58.3% of the dispensable genes and 33.9% for the core genes set could not be assigned any functional annotation. Moreover, 95.5% of core genes had homologs in other species based on BLAST searches to 32 plant genomes (excluding soybean), significantly more than the dispensable gene set (83.5%, chi-squared test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01). These results confirm that lineage-specific genes evolve faster than genes that are shared between species, either by means of a higher evolutionary rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Cai, J.J. &amp; Petrov, D.A. Relaxed purifying selection and possibly high rate of adaptation in primate lineage-specific genes. Genome Biol. Evol. 2, 393â409 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2317">22</a></sup> or a higher gene loss rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Krylov, D.M., Wolf, Y.I., Rogozin, I.B. &amp; Koonin, E.V. Gene loss, protein sequence divergence, gene dispensability, expression level, and interactivity are correlated in eukaryotic evolution. Genome Res. 13, 2229â2235 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2321">23</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6"><i>G. soja</i> versus <i>G. max</i>: genomic basis of agronomic traits</h3><p>R-genes with NBS domains mediate effector-triggered immunity acting as detectors for pathogen virulence proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Innes, R.W. et al. Differential accumulation of retroelements and diversification of NB-LRR disease resistance genes in duplicated regions following polyploidy in the ancestor of soybean. Plant Physiol. 148, 1740â1759 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2339">24</a></sup>. The quantity of R-genes with a given domain architecture and the number of unique domain architectures varied between the two species. <i>G. soja</i> contained more R-gene domain architectures (25) than <i>G. max</i> (14) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). The 11 lineage-specific R-gene domain architectures found in <i>G. soja</i> accessions were part of the dispensable genome, possibly reflecting adaptation to biotic stresses. The <i>G. soja</i> genome contained a broader range of NBS R-gene domain architectures than did <i>G. max</i>, but <i>G. max</i> had more NBS-encoding genes (460 versus 334 in GsojaA to 382 in GsojaD) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 22</a>). R-genes showed more copy number loss in <i>G. soja</i> accessions (88 lost versus 33 gained and 8 copy number gain/loss, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 23</a>), several of which were known to confer resistance to soybean mosaic virus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wen, R.H., Khatabi, B., Ashfield, T., Maroof, M.S. &amp; Hajimorad, M. The HC-Pro and P3 cistrons of an avirulent soybean mosaic virus are recognized by different resistance genes at the complex Rsv1 locus. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 26, 203â215 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2375">25</a></sup>, Asian soybean rust<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Monteros, M.J., Ha, B.-K., Phillips, D.V. &amp; Boerma, H.R. SNP assay to detect the 'Hyuuga'red-brown lesion resistance gene for Asian soybean rust. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 1023â1032 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2379">26</a></sup> and <i>Phytophthora</i> root rot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, J. et al. Genetic characterization and fine mapping of the novel Phytophthora resistance gene in a Chinese soybean cultivar. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 1555â1561 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2386">27</a></sup>, and be involved in legume-rhizobia symbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yang, S., Tang, F., Gao, M., Krishnan, H.B. &amp; Zhu, H. R gene-controlled host specificity in the legumeârhizobia symbiosis. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18735â18740 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2390">28</a></sup>. In addition, five structurally diverged regions (located on chromosomes 03, 06, 07 and 18) within cultivated <i>G. max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="McHale, L.K. et al. Structural variants in the soybean genome localize to clusters of biotic stress-response genes. Plant Physiol. 159, 1295â1308 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2397">29</a></sup>, had a high frequency of CNV in the pan-genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Variation of resistance genes and flowering timeârelated genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Variation of resistance genes and flowering timeârelated genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2979/MediaObjects/41587_2014_Article_BFnbt2979_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1020"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Heatmap for CNV of resistance-related PFAM gene categories across all accessions. Copy number ratio of gene categories in each box was calculated as the number of genes in each <i>G. soja</i> accession over number of genes in GmaxW82 within the same gene category. Red represents greater gene numbers in <i>G. soja</i> than GmaxW82, whereas green indicates fewer. Gene numbers for GmaxW82 for the first 14 gene categories are shown on left. In categories where GmaxW82 does not have any genes (gene number = 0), black indicates the absence of genes in a given <i>G. soja</i> accession, while red indicates the presence of genes in <i>G. soja</i> vs. none in GmaxW82. (<b>b</b>) Large-effect mutations and new gene copy in flowering timeârelated genes. In <i>PHYA4</i>, <i>E1</i> and <i>LFY</i>, amino acid changes resulting from large-effect SNPs and indels are shown. In the <i>FT</i> gene family, a new copy named <i>FT2c</i> was found in all seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2979/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Genes controlling flowering time were likely involved in adaptation to new climatic regions. Comparing the <i>G. soja</i> genomes and GmaxW82, flowering time-related genes with large-effect variation were identified, including light receptor <i>PHYA</i>, floral integrator <i>FLOWERING LOCUS T</i> (<i>FT</i>) and meristem identity protein <i>LEAFY</i> (<i>LFY</i>) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Two of the four homologs of <i>PHYA</i> in soybean, <i>GmPHYA2</i> and <i>GmPHYA3</i>, correspond to major flowering loci, <i>E4</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Liu, B. et al. Genetic redundancy in soybean photoresponses associated with duplication of the phytochrome A gene. Genetics 180, 995â1007 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2499">30</a>) and <i>E3</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Watanabe, S. et al. Map-based cloning of the gene associated with the soybean maturity locus E3. Genetics 182, 1251â1262 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2505">31</a>), respectively. We found mutations in <i>GmPHYA4</i> (<i>Glyma03g38620</i>), resulting in changes and deletions of amino acid (AA) in the N-terminal P3/GAF domain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Figs. 17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">18</a>). Among ten <i>FT</i> homologs in soybean, <i>GmFT2c</i> showed the closest relationship to <i>GmFT2a</i>, a candidate gene that coordinates flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kong, F. et al. Two coordinately regulated homologs of FLOWERING LOCUS T are involved in the control of photoperiodic flowering in soybean. Plant Physiol. 154, 1220â1231 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2531">32</a></sup>. In GmaxW82, <i>GmFT2c</i> was missing <span class="stix">â¼</span>50% of the N-terminal peptides (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>); in <i>G. soja</i>, however, <i>GmFT2c</i> had a complete gene structure and was expressed. <i>LFY</i>, which regulates the timing of floral induction in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, had two putative orthologous copies in GmaxW82 [<i>GmLFY1</i> (<i>Glyma04g37900</i>) and <i>GmLFY2</i> (<i>Glyma06g17170</i>)]. Two 3-bp indels and nine nonsynonymous SNPs were identified in <i>GmLFY1</i> among the seven <i>G. soja</i> accessions, all in exons 1 and 2, forming variation hotpots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Both <i>GmFT2c</i> and <i>GmLFY1</i> were located in or near QTL, controlling the period of reproductive growth stage and maturity time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Xin, D.W. et al. Analysis of quantitative trait loci underlying the period of reproductive growth stages in soybean (Glycine max [L.] Merr.). Euphytica 162, 155â165 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2586">33</a></sup>, thus providing gene candidates that may underlie flowering time in soybean.</p><p>Oil and fatty acid content of soybean has been intensely selected such that <i>G. max</i> produces nearly twice as much oil as <i>G. soja</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Xu, B. et al. A study on fat content and fatty acid composition of wild soybean (G. soja) in China. Jinlin Agric. Sci. 2, 1â6 (1993)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2598">34</a></sup>. A total of 1,332 genes were annotated in GmaxW82 based on homology to genes in 24 acyl lipid subpathways in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 24</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li-Beisson, Y. et al. Acyl-lipid metabolism. The Arabidopsis Book 11, e0161 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2608">35</a></sup>. Of these genes, 15.9% contained CNV and/or large-effect SNP and/or indels between the <i>G. soja</i> accessions and GmaxW82. Although five subpathways exhibited a significantly low frequency of variation (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, Fisher's exact test; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 24</a>), a number of gene variants were identified. Fatty acids are precursors for jasmonate production in the oxylipin pathway in plants following stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kachroo, A. &amp; Kachroo, P. Fatty acid-derived signals in plant defense. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 47, 153â176 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2622">36</a></sup>. <i>SAG101</i> (<i>senescence-associated gene101</i>) in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, which encodes a triacylglycerol lipase to hydrolyze triacylglycerol to monoacylglycerol and produce free fatty acid, plays important roles in plant innate immunity against biotrophic pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Feys, B.J. et al. Arabidopsis SENESCENCE-ASSOCIATED GENE101 stabilizes and signals within an ENHANCED DISEASE SUSCEPTIBILITY1 complex in plant innate immunity. Plant Cell 17, 2601â2613 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2636">37</a></sup>. In this study, large-effect SNP and/or indels were found in three clustered homologs of <i>SAG101</i> (<i>Glyma13g04561</i>, <i>Glyma13g04651</i> and <i>Glyma13g04571</i>) and are candidate genes for nearby QTL that affect oil content and confer resistance to <i>Phytophthora sojae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Qi, Z.M. et al. Soybean oil content QTL mapping and integrating with meta-analysis method for mining genes. Euphytica 179, 499â514 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2655">38</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>).</p><p>Triacylglycerol is a major storage lipid in soybean and acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (DGAT) is a key enzyme that catalyzes diacylglycerol to triacylglycerol, determining seed oil content in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li-Beisson, Y. et al. Acyl-lipid metabolism. The Arabidopsis Book 11, e0161 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2667">35</a></sup>. Among 113 potential triacylglycerol biosynthesis genes, 10 had putative loss-of-function frameshifts due to large-effect SNP and/or indels, including the <i>DGAT</i> homolog <i>(Glyma16g21960</i>, <i>GmDGAT2B</i>) and a homolog of <i>Abscisic Acid Insensitive 4</i> (<i>ABI4</i>) (<i>Glyma14g06290</i>), a transcription factor that regulates triacylglycerol synthesis in <i>Arabidopsis</i> by activating <i>DGAT1</i> expression during nitrogen deficiency (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yang, Y., Yu, X., Song, L. &amp; An, C. ABI4 activates DGAT1 expression in Arabidopsis seedlings during nitrogen deficiency. Plant Physiol. 156, 873â883 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2699">39</a></sup>. A frame-shift mutation in <i>GmDGAT2B</i> was previously found to be associated with reduced seed oil concentration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Eskandari, M., Cober, E.R. &amp; Rajcan, I. Using the candidate gene approach for detecting genes underlying seed oil concentration and yield in soybean. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 1839â1850 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2707">40</a></sup>. Another <i>DGAT</i> homolog, <i>DGAT1B</i> (<i>Glyma17g06120</i>), negatively associated with seed protein content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Eskandari, M., Cober, E.R. &amp; Rajcan, I. Using the candidate gene approach for detecting genes underlying seed oil concentration and yield in soybean. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 1839â1850 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2720">40</a></sup>, was found to have been positively selected during domestication (protein and oil content are negatively correlated).</p><p>In contrast to <i>G. soja</i>, cultivated soybean has larger organ sizes, thicker stems and shorter plant height (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). A QTL region controlling plant height, lodging and yield spans <span class="stix">â¼</span>1.2Mbp on chromosome 9 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wang, D., Graef, G., Procopiuk, A. &amp; Diers, B. Identification of putative QTL that underlie yield in interspecific soybean backcross populations. Theor. Appl. Genet. 108, 458â467 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2733">41</a>). In this region, four genes were identified with large-effect SNP and/or indels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2979#MOESM12">Supplementary Table 25</a>). GO analysis implicated all four genes in plant development, and work in other species has shown these genes to be either directly or indirectly involved in cytokinin metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ashikari, M. et al. Cytokinin oxidase regulates rice grain production. Science 309, 741â745 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2740">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Johnson, K. &amp; Lenhard, M. Genetic control of plant organ growth. New Phytol. 191, 319â333 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2743">43</a></sup>. The identification of large-effect SNP and/or indels between <i>G. soja</i> and GmaxW82 can facilitate discovery and cloning of candidate genes related to the domestication and improvement of soybean.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Our work illustrates the advantage of <i>de novo</i> assembly in detecting genetic variation that would not have been found by resequencing alone. The majority (94%) of <i>G. max</i> genes were covered by our <i>G. soja</i> assemblies. Thus, we were able to identify structural variation, including 338 PAV, 1,978 CNV, and a series of SNPs and indels in highly divergent genic regions, where only assembled sequences could be mapped. Using this data set, we estimated that the divergence time between <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max</i> was nearly three times earlier than a previous estimate based on a single <i>G. soja</i> accession<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2779">4</a></sup>.</p><p>The individual genomes and the pan-genome could be improved by additional sequencing of larger insert libraries to increase contig and scaffold sizes, which would allow full exploration of the nongenic parts of the genome. These improvements would also enable further interrogation of chromosome-scale structural variation, such as inversion and translocation events. Given the structural complexity of plant genomes, further advances in sequencing technology and bioinformatics tools are needed to overcome difficulties in genome assembly and annotation to deal with highly similar transposable elements and other abundant repetitive sequences, heterozygosity and polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Claros, M.G. et al. Why assembling plant genome sequences is so challenging. Biology 1, 439â459 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2786">44</a></sup>. Furthermore, the concept of a pan-genome should be expanded beyond the DNA level to include variation in gene regulation and expression.</p><p>This study confirms that a single genome does not adequately represent the diversity contained within a species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Genetic diversity in domesticated soybean (Glycine max) and its wild progenitor (Glycine soja) for simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphism loci. New Phytol. 188, 242â253 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2793">8</a></sup>. We constructed a <i>de novo</i> assembly-based pan-genome of a crop or crop wild relatives, by sequencing seven representative wild soybean accessions from East Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, Y.H. et al. Genetic diversity in domesticated soybean (Glycine max) and its wild progenitor (Glycine soja) for simple sequence repeat and single-nucleotide polymorphism loci. New Phytol. 188, 242â253 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2800">8</a></sup>, the center of <i>G. soja</i> diversity and domestication center of <i>G. max</i>. The <i>G. soja</i> pan-genome was 30.2 Mbp larger than the genome of a single resequenced accession<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kim, M.Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2814">4</a></sup>. The number of accessions required to represent the majority of genetic variation within a species is case-dependent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Medini, D., Donati, C., Tettelin, H., Masignani, V. &amp; Rappuoli, R. The microbial pan-genome. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 15, 589â594 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2818">45</a></sup>, and cataloguing a pan-genome will never be complete. The observation that the <i>G. soja</i> pan-genome size began to level off suggests that we have captured a representative portion of the species' gene pool. Sequencing additional samples may further reveal allelic structure and variation within and between populations, thereby allowing investigation into local adaptation and migration. Increasing the sample size may also add to the dispensable genome, which is characterized by greater variation than the core genome and is potentially involved in environmental adaptation and organismal interactions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Read, B.A. et al. Pan genome of the phytoplankton Emiliania underpins its global distribution. Nature 499, 209â213 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2825">46</a></sup>. Approximately 20% of the <i>G. soja</i> sequences and 51.4% of gene families were found to be dispensable, an interesting juxtaposition to the recently published pan-transcriptome of 503 maize samples where 82.7% of the representative transcript assemblies were dispensable<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hirsch, C.N. et al. Insights into the maize pan-genome and pan-transcriptome. Plant Cell 26, 121â135 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2833">47</a></sup>, supporting the prediction that an outbred species such as maize would have a larger dispensable genome than an autogamous species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Medini, D., Donati, C., Tettelin, H., Masignani, V. &amp; Rappuoli, R. The microbial pan-genome. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 15, 589â594 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2837">45</a></sup>.</p><p>The pan-genome of <i>G. soja</i> shows the extent of novel genes and alleles in wild relatives that can be introgressed into crops, which typically have lower genetic diversity as the result of domestication and breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hyten, D.L. et al. Impacts of genetic bottlenecks on soybean genome diversity. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 16666â16671 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2847">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Doebley, J.F., Gaut, B.S. &amp; Smith, B.D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell 127, 1309â1321 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2979#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d47811e2850">49</a></sup>. Candidate genes associated with adaptation to various abiotic and biotic stresses may contribute to increased resilience to climate variability in cultivated soybean. Genes with structural variation and large-effect variation between <i>G. soja</i> and <i>G. max</i> (GmaxW82) that associate with agronomic phenotypes, as inferred from homology and comparison with mapped QTLs, can be used to develop molecular markers for these segments and test new allelic combinations.
